<article id="post-5251" class="post-5251 post type-post status-publish format-standard hentry category-january-in-japan category-japan category-natsume-soseki category-review">
	<header class="entry-header">

		<h2 class="entry-title"><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2016/02/18/the-heredity-of-taste-by-natsume-soseki-review/" rel="bookmark">&#8216;The Heredity of Taste&#8217; by Natsume Sōseki&nbsp;(Review)</a></h2>
					<div class="entry-meta">
				<span class="posted-on"><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2016/02/18/the-heredity-of-taste-by-natsume-soseki-review/" rel="bookmark"><time class="entry-date published" datetime="2016-02-18T16:00:14+00:00">February 18, 2016</time><time class="updated" datetime="2016-02-18T15:56:53+00:00">February 18, 2016</time></a></span><span class="byline"><span class="author vcard"><a class="url fn n" href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/author/tonysreadinglist/">Tony</a></span></span><span class="comments-link"><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2016/02/18/the-heredity-of-taste-by-natsume-soseki-review/#comments">3 Comments</a></span>			</div><!-- .entry-meta -->
		
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	
		<div class="entry-content">
			<p><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.files.wordpress.com/2016/02/img_5391.jpg" rel="attachment wp-att-5253"><img class="size-medium wp-image-5253 alignleft" src="https://tonysreadinglist.files.wordpress.com/2016/02/img_5391.jpg?w=300&#038;h=225" alt="IMG_5391" width="300" height="225" /></a>There&#8217;s a point at which interest in a writer crosses the line into obsession, and in the case of <strong><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/category/natsume-soseki/">Natsume Sōseki</a></strong> that point was reached a long, long time ago.  In addition to reading, and rereading, his more famous books (e.g. <em><strong><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2010/08/17/review-post-41-hey-teacher-leave-those-kids-a-no-actually-give-them-a-kicking-they-deserve-it/">Botchan</a></strong></em>, <em><strong><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2010/05/04/review-post-20-i-am-most-definitely-not-a-cat/">I am a Cat</a></strong></em>, <a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2011/04/21/yet-another-taste-of-japan/"><em><strong>Kokoro</strong></em></a>), I&#8217;ve spent countless hours online looking out for any new releases (e.g. the recent <strong>Aardvark Bureau</strong> edition of <em><strong><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2015/09/10/the-miner-by-natsume-soseki-review/">The Miner</a></strong></em>) and searching for second-hand editions of his minor works.  Today&#8217;s choice is a <em>very</em> minor early work, one I doubt would appeal to most readers, but for any Sōseki fan there&#8217;s always something there to make reading his work worthwhile &#8211; let&#8217;s see if I can find it <span class='wp-smiley wp-emoji wp-emoji-smile' title=':)'>:)</span></p>
<p>*****<br />
<em><strong>The Heredity of Taste</strong></em> (translated by <strong>Sammy I. Tsunematsu</strong>, published by <strong>Tuttle Publishing</strong>), set in Tokyo in 1905, is a novella in three parts focusing on the recently ended Russo-Japanese war and its effects on one family.  The story begins with the narrator strolling through the streets on his way to a meeting at Shimbara railway station, idly musing on the idea of war as he goes:</p>
<p><em><strong>Under the influence of the weather, even the gods lose their reason.  &#8220;Let&#8217;s exterminate mankind!  Let loose the ravenous dogs!&#8221; was the cry that resounded from the heavens to the depths of the Sea of Japan and made it rage in all directions.  The cry penetrated as far as Manchuria.  As soon as they heard it, the Japanese and Russians responded by creating an immense slaughterhouse in the plains to the north of the Continent of Asia, stretching over more than 400 kilometers.  So, under the skies, great hordes of ferocious dogs appeared and sped across the vast expanse.</strong></em><br />
<strong>p.17 (Tuttle Publishing, 2004)</strong></p>
<p>Ironically, he is roused from his gloomy thoughts by a crowd of people with a rather different opinion of the war, relatives of the returning victorious Japanese troops waiting to welcome their sons and fathers back to the mother country.</p>
<p>When the narrator decides to take a look at the troops as they file past, tired, dishevelled and burnt by the fierce continental sun, he notices one soldier in particular, a young man who reminds him of a friend of his, Kō-san &#8211; one who tragically lost his life over in China.  This pricks his conscience, sending him off to the cemetery to pay his respects, where he sees a young woman laying flowers on his friend&#8217;s grave.  She departs before our friend is able to gather his wits, leaving him wondering who she could be.  As an only son, with no wife or fiancée, the dearly departed had no young woman in his life at all &#8211; at least, not until now&#8230;</p>
<p><em>The Heredity of Taste</em> is described as an anti-war novel, and this is most evident in the first half of the book.  The narrator imagines the Japanese attack on a Russian stronghold, a vision of a line of soldiers, moving forwards like ants.  These are melancholy thoughts of whole units gunned down in the trench surrounding the fortress, never to arise, an image in stark contrast to the jingoistic &#8216;Banzai&#8217; cries at Shimbara station.</p>
<p>However, the story soon moves on from the epic to the intimate, with much of the second half spent pursuing the mystery woman.  The narrator takes it upon himself to find out who she is, visiting Kō-san&#8217;s mother and asking for her son&#8217;s diary in which he hopes to find clues as to the woman&#8217;s identity.  Part of the impetus for the quest is pure curiosity, his interest piqued by the fleeting glimpse of a beautiful woman, yet there&#8217;s a more serious motive behind it too.  His desire to find out the truth is an attempt to make right what was destroyed by the war, hopefully giving the dead hero&#8217;s mother something to ease her pain.</p>
<p>In truth, this is not Sōseki&#8217;s best work.  It&#8217;s a slight, 80-page novella, a story caught between a mixture of styles which never really cohere.  The title is related to the secret of the woman and the relationship between two families, but this idea of inherited tastes has little to do with the other ideas of the story and seems slightly misplaced.  Apparently, <em>The Heredity of Taste</em> was written in eight days, and, well, I&#8217;m quite prepared to believe it&#8230;</p>
<p>The story was published in 1906, one of four books released that year (according to <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Natsume_S%C5%8Dseki#Major_works">Sōseki&#8217;s Wikipedia page</a>, anyway&#8230;).  It follows <em>Botchan</em> and <a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2010/11/20/a-grass-pillow-for-my-head/"><em><strong>Kusamakura</strong></em></a>, preceding <em><strong><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2013/01/17/the-210th-day-by-natsume-soseki-review/">The 210th Day</a></strong></em>, and the dry humour of these early books is evident here too:</p>
<p><strong><em>Understanding that the best thing to do is keep quiet and resolutely adopting that policy, I steered well clear of such comments.  I am an honest man by nature, but as I live in society I try not to arouse anyone&#8217;s resentment and sometimes I am compelled to lie in this endeavor.  As soon as honesty becomes compatible with daily social life, I will stop telling such lies. </em>(pp.73/4)</strong></p>
<p>Typical early Sōseki &#8211; words which could have come straight from the mouth of a certain cat <span class='wp-smiley wp-emoji wp-emoji-wink' title=';)'>;)</span></p>
<p>For me (and, I suspect, for many other Sōseki admirers) the main interest of <em>The Heredity of Taste</em> lies in its relation to other, later work.  One book which certainly uses ideas first mentioned here is <em>Kokoro</em>.  This one too is a three-part story, and both works chronicle a friend&#8217;s demise and have a woman at the heart of the story.  Another theme repeated later in Sōseki&#8217;s career is that of the suicide of the army General after the Meiji Emperor&#8217;s death: here it&#8217;s a minor detail, but in <em>Kokoro</em>, it&#8217;s an important part of the story, playing a part in the main character&#8217;s own decisions.</p>
<p>For readers with little knowledge of the era, <strong>Stephen W. Kohl&#8217;s</strong> introduction fills in some cultural and historical gaps, while also highlighting the importance of the Russo-Japanese War for Japan (a conflict marking the first time an Asian nation had defeated a European power).  Kohl remarks on Sōseki&#8217;s approach of favouring the micro view over the macro:</p>
<p><strong><em>This story may be added to the list of Sōseki&#8217;s stories of failed love, yet the poignancy of the failure of these two young people to be together in a loving relationship is an expression of the losses suffered during the war.  Instead of telling us how many thousands of young men died at Port Arthur, he shows us the full, tragic implications of a single death and leaves it to us as readers to multiply this tragedy by uncounted thousands.</em> (p.15)</strong></p>
<p>How successfully he does this is up for debate, and I can&#8217;t really see many readers now rushing out to source a copy.  However, if there any other Sōseki tragics out there, I&#8217;m sure you&#8217;ll enjoy finding out for yourselves <span class='wp-smiley wp-emoji wp-emoji-wink' title=';)'>;)</span></p>
					</div><!-- .entry-content -->

	
	<footer class="entry-meta entry-footer">
		<span class="cat-links"><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/category/january-in-japan/" rel="category tag">January in Japan</a>, <a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/category/japan/" rel="category tag">Japan</a>, <a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/category/natsume-soseki/" rel="category tag">Natsume Soseki</a>, <a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/category/review/" rel="category tag">Review</a></span>	</footer><!-- .entry-footer -->
</article><!-- #post-5251 -->
			
				
<article id="post-5237" class="post-5237 post type-post status-publish format-standard hentry category-bloomsbury-australia category-france category-patrick-modiano category-review">
	<header class="entry-header">

		<h2 class="entry-title"><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2016/02/15/the-night-watch-by-patrick-modiano-review/" rel="bookmark">&#8216;The Night Watch&#8217; by Patrick Modiano&nbsp;(Review)</a></h2>
					<div class="entry-meta">
				<span class="posted-on"><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2016/02/15/the-night-watch-by-patrick-modiano-review/" rel="bookmark"><time class="entry-date published" datetime="2016-02-15T18:00:43+00:00">February 15, 2016</time><time class="updated" datetime="2016-02-15T15:10:24+00:00">February 15, 2016</time></a></span><span class="byline"><span class="author vcard"><a class="url fn n" href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/author/tonysreadinglist/">Tony</a></span></span><span class="comments-link"><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2016/02/15/the-night-watch-by-patrick-modiano-review/#comments">6 Comments</a></span>			</div><!-- .entry-meta -->
		
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	
		<div class="entry-content">
			<p><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.files.wordpress.com/2015/10/img_5331.jpg" rel="attachment wp-att-4627"><img class="size-medium wp-image-4627 alignleft" src="https://tonysreadinglist.files.wordpress.com/2015/10/img_5331.jpg?w=300&#038;h=225" alt="IMG_5331" width="300" height="225" /></a>With yet more translations of <a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/category/patrick-modiano/"><strong>Patrick Modiano&#8217;s</strong></a> work appearing this year (a couple of which I already have on the shelves), it&#8217;s high time I read more of what appeared in 2015.  Last year, I posted on the first of <em>The Occupation Trilogy</em> novels, collected in <a href="http://www.bloomsbury.com/au/the-occupation-trilogy-9781408867907/">a beautiful hardback edition from <strong>Bloomsbury Publishing</strong></a>, and today I&#8217;m looking at the second of these short works from early in Modiano&#8217;s career.  There&#8217;s a definite connection in style with the exuberant debut of <em><strong><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2015/10/29/la-place-de-letoile-by-patrick-modiano-review/">La Place de l&#8217;Étoile</a></strong></em>, yet if you look closely, the transition to his later, more minimalist technique is slowly getting underway&#8230;</p>
<p>*****<br />
<em><strong>The Night Watch</strong></em> (translated by <strong>Patricia Wolf</strong>, revised by <strong>Frank Wynne</strong>) begins with a bang, throwing the reader into a hedonistic scene with hints of 1920s Berlin (or <em><strong>The Great Gatsby</strong></em>).  It&#8217;s actually occupied Paris, and an unlikely mix of crooks and fake aristocracy are fiddling while French self-esteem burns:</p>
<p><em><strong>&#8216;One rumba, just one rumba,&#8217; pleads the Baroness. &#8216;One rumba, one rumba!&#8217; shrieks Violette Morris.  Beneath the glow of the chandeliers, they flush, turning blue in the face, flushing to deep purple.  Beads of perspiration trickle down their temples, their eyes grow wide.  Pols de Helder&#8217;s face grows black as if it were burning up.  Count Baruzzi&#8217;s cheeks are sunken, the bags under Rachid von Rosenheim&#8217;s eyes puff bloated.  Lionel de Zieff brings one hand to his heart.  Costachesco and Odicharvi seem stupified.  The women&#8217;s make-up begins to crack, their hair turning ever more garish colours.  They are all putrefying and will surely rot right where they stand.  Do they stink already?</strong></em><br />
<strong>p.123 (Bloomsbury, 2015)</strong></p>
<p>A chaotic scene, one in which the reader struggles to find their bearings, forced to latch on to the narrator (although he&#8217;s less a narrator here than a silent pair of eyes drinking in the carnage).</p>
<p>When the party dies down, we slowly begin to make sense of the story.  The chaotic troupe are a bunch of criminals made-good, taking advantage of the flight from Paris of the city&#8217;s rulers to take control of the city, cooperating with the (largely unseen and unmentioned) German occupiers.  Having been discovered by one of the group&#8217;s leaders, the young narrator, known only by the code name of Swing Troubador, becomes a handy henchman, favoured for his knowledge of culture (allowing him to recognise the loot worth plundering from the vacant mansions) and his pleasing, innocent countenance.</p>
<p>This feature is to lead him into darkness as he is used by the gang to infiltrate a resistance group, led by &#8216;the Lieutenant&#8217;.  At the same time, the freedom fighters assign him the name of La Princess de Lamballe and task him with bringing down the leaders of the traitors bringing fear to the French capital.  Obviously, there&#8217;s only so long he can string both groups along &#8211; and whichever way he jumps, there&#8217;s unlikely to be a happy ending.</p>
<p>In <em>The Night Watch</em>, Modiano once again sets out to poke away at the sore wound of French behaviour during the Second World War occupation, with the shadowy youth at the center of the novel a reflection of the average citizen&#8217;s behaviour during the period:</p>
<p><strong><em>It was in the pawnshop on the Rue Pierre Charron (my mother would often go there, but they always refused to take her paste jewellery) that I decided once and for all that poverty was a pain in the arse.  You might think I have no principles.  I stared out a pure and innocent soul.  But innocence gets lost along the way.</em> (p.171)</strong></p>
<p>The youth is neither good nor bad, a drifter shaped by circumstances, but he is marked by a distinct lack of a real moral centre, unable to decide between the two groups who put their trust in him, and the temptation of wealth and relative comfort is too much for him.</p>
<p>Which is not to say that he throws himself obliviously into his role as a double agent.  There are frequent glimpses of his sense of shame at this betrayal, and he&#8217;s under no illusion that his actions might all turn out for the best.  It is made clear that these long, violent nights are temporary and that the old order will eventually be restored.  In fact, he shows a sober sense of realism, knowing that his time will come, all the bright lights burning out in the darkness of a bloody Parisian night.</p>
<p>These reflective moments, especially in the middle of the book, show far more of Modiano&#8217;s later sparse style than was evident in <em>La Place de l&#8217;Étoile</em>, and it appears that a shift away from the more sarcastic, parodic writing is already underway.  However, these brief oases of calm are sandwiched between the deliberately confusing scenes back at the HQ (a requisitioned mansion), where drink, drugs, nudity and loud music are juxtaposed with the screams of tortured resistance fighters coming from the basement.  Just as in Modiano&#8217;s debut work, the reader is swept up in a dizzying, confusing world, one in which anything seems to go.  The major difference between <em>The Night Watch</em> and <em>La Place de l&#8217;Étoile</em>, though, is that this time the effect  is more fleeting, and we are given time to reflect on the characters&#8217; behaviour &#8211; and for the original French readership, with memories of the war still fairly fresh, on their own.</p>
<p><em>The Night Watch</em> doesn&#8217;t quite run to 100 pages, but it&#8217;s an interesting, complex read, a novella showcasing a slight shift in the writer&#8217;s style, and another story holding a mirror up to his countrymen, asking them if they recognise themselves there.  Many will deny any resemblance, but some might have pause for thought, just like the unfortunate protagonist.  Much as he would like to claim a distinction between himself and the people he serves, they gently set him right:</p>
<p><em><strong>&#8216;I&#8217;m only too aware that all this might seem rather distasteful to a well-bred young man.  But&#8230;&#8217; &#8211; his voice takes on a savage tone &#8211; &#8216;the fact that you find yourself among such disreputable souls tonight means that, despite that choirboy face of yours,&#8217; (very tenderly) &#8216;we belong to the same world, Monsieur.&#8217; </strong></em><strong>(p.149)</strong></p>
					</div><!-- .entry-content -->

	
	<footer class="entry-meta entry-footer">
		<span class="cat-links"><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/category/bloomsbury-australia/" rel="category tag">Bloomsbury Australia</a>, <a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/category/france/" rel="category tag">France</a>, <a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/category/patrick-modiano/" rel="category tag">Patrick Modiano</a>, <a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/category/review/" rel="category tag">Review</a></span>	</footer><!-- .entry-footer -->

<article id="post-5275" class="post-5275 post type-post status-publish format-standard hentry category-man-booker-international-prize-2016">
	<header class="entry-header">

		<h2 class="entry-title"><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2016/02/14/introducing-the-2016-mbip-shadow-panel/" rel="bookmark">Introducing the 2016 MBIP Shadow&nbsp;Panel!</a></h2>
					<div class="entry-meta">
				<span class="posted-on"><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2016/02/14/introducing-the-2016-mbip-shadow-panel/" rel="bookmark"><time class="entry-date published" datetime="2016-02-14T12:00:54+00:00">February 14, 2016</time><time class="updated" datetime="2016-02-14T12:16:54+00:00">February 14, 2016</time></a></span><span class="byline"><span class="author vcard"><a class="url fn n" href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/author/tonysreadinglist/">Tony</a></span></span><span class="comments-link"><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2016/02/14/introducing-the-2016-mbip-shadow-panel/#comments">15 Comments</a></span>			</div><!-- .entry-meta -->
		
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	
		<div class="entry-content">
			<p><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.files.wordpress.com/2016/02/mbip-2016.jpg" rel="attachment wp-att-5184"><img class="size-full wp-image-5184 alignleft" src="https://tonysreadinglist.files.wordpress.com/2016/02/mbip-2016.jpg?w=636" alt="MBIP 2016"   /></a>With March drawing ever closer, those of you in the northern hemisphere are, no doubt, looking forward to warmer weather (while we in the south make the most of the last days of summer&#8230;).  However, quite apart from the joys of spring/autumn, there&#8217;s a lot to look forward to over the coming months with the <strong>2016 Man Booker International Prize</strong>, the first edition of the revamped prize taking over from the <strong>International Foreign Fiction Prize</strong>,  finally springing (!) into action.</p>
<p>Last week, <a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2016/02/07/man-booker-international-prize-2016-predictions/">I put forward a few suggestions as to what might be on the longlist</a>, but this week it&#8217;s finally time to unveil a rather different list, namely the people who will be watching from the shadows, scrutinising the decisions of those on the real panel, and (most likely) frowning at their foolishness.  Ladies and gentlemen: here is your <strong>2016 Shadow Panel</strong>!</p>
<p>*****<br />
<b>Stu Allen</b> is returning to chair the first Man Booker International Prize shadow jury after hosting four shadow IFFP juries.  He blogs out of <a href="https://winstonsdad.wordpress.com/"><b>Winstonsdad’s Blog</b></a>, home to 500-plus translated books in review.  He can be found on twitter (<b>@stujallen</b>), where he also started the successful translated fiction hashtag <b>#TranslationThurs</b> over five years ago.</p>
<p><b>Tony Malone</b> is an Anglo-Australian reviewer with a particular focus on German, Japanese and Korean fiction.  He blogs at <a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/"><b>Tony&#8217;s Reading List</b></a>, and his reviews have also appeared at <i>Words Without Borders</i>, <i>Necessary Fiction</i> and <i>Shiny New Books</i>.  Based in Melbourne, he teaches ESL to prospective university students when he&#8217;s not reading and reviewing.  He can also be found on Twitter <b>@tony_malone</b></p>
<p><b>Clare</b> started blogging at <a href="http://alittleblogofbooks.com/"><b>A Little Blog of Books</b></a> four years ago. When she&#8217;s not doing her day job in London, she blogs mostly about contemporary literary fiction and particularly enjoys reading books by French and Japanese authors. Twitter: <b>@littleblogbooks</b></p>
<p><b>Tony Messenger</b> is addicted to lists, and books &#8211; put the two together (especially translated works) and the bookshelves sigh under the weight of new purchases as the &#8220;to be read&#8221; piles grow and the voracious all-night reading continues. Another Tony from Melbourne Australia, <b>@Messy_tony</b> (his Twitter handle) may sometimes be mistaken for the more famous Malone Tony but rest assured they&#8217;re two different people. Messy Tony can be found at <a href="http://messybooker.blogspot.com.au/"><b>Messengers Booker (and more)</b></a> and at <b>Messenger&#8217;s Booker</b> on Facebook &#8211; with a blog containing the word &#8220;booker&#8221; why wouldn&#8217;t he read this list?</p>
<p><b>Lori Feathers</b> lives in Dallas, Texas, and is a freelance book critic and member of the National Book Critics Circle.  Her recent reviews can be found at <i>Words Without Borders, Full Stop, World Literature Today, Three Percent, Rain Taxi </i>and on Twitter <b>@LoriFeathers</b></p>
<p><b>Bellezza</b> is a blogger from Chicago, Illinois, who has been writing <a href="http://dolcebellezza.net/"><b>Dolce Bellezza</b></a> for ten years. She has run the <b>Japanese Literature Challenge</b> for 9 years, and her reviews can be found on publisher sites such as Penguin Random House, Simon and Schuster, Peirene Press, and SoHo Press. It is her great joy to participate in the shadow jury for the Man Booker International Prize with fellow participants who are experts in translated literature.</p>
<p><b>David Hebblethwaite</b> is a book blogger and reviewer from the north of England, now based in the south. He has written about translated fiction for <i>Words Without Borders</i>, <i>Shiny New Books</i>, <i>Strange Horizons</i>, and <i>We Love This Book</i>. He blogs at <a href="http://www.davidsbookworld.com/"><b>David&#8217;s Book World</b></a> and tweets as <b>@David_Heb</b>.</p>
<p><b>Grant Rintoul</b> is a Scottish reviewer who lives on the coast not far from the 39 steps said to have inspired Buchan’s novel. Luckily the weather is generally ideal for reading. He blogs at <a href="https://1streading.wordpress.com/"><b>1streading</b></a>, so-called as he rarely has time to look at anything twice. He can sometimes be found on Twitter <b>@GrantRintoul</b></p>
<p>*****<br />
So, having introduced the brave souls who will be shadowing the real judges, all that remains is to see what books we&#8217;ll be reading.  The longlist announcement is only a few weeks away now &#8211; and then the fun and games can really begin!</p>
					</div><!-- .entry-content -->

	
	<footer class="entry-meta entry-footer">
		<span class="cat-links"><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/category/man-booker-international-prize-2016/" rel="category tag">Man Booker International Prize 2016</a></span>	</footer><!-- .entry-footer -->
</article><!-- #post-5275 -->
			
				
<article id="post-5229" class="post-5229 post type-post status-publish format-standard hentry category-january-in-japan category-japan category-review category-yukio-mishima">
	<header class="entry-header">

		<h2 class="entry-title"><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2016/02/12/the-sound-of-waves-by-yukio-mishima-review/" rel="bookmark">&#8216;The Sound of Waves&#8217; by Yukio Mishima&nbsp;(Review)</a></h2>
					<div class="entry-meta">
				<span class="posted-on"><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2016/02/12/the-sound-of-waves-by-yukio-mishima-review/" rel="bookmark"><time class="entry-date published" datetime="2016-02-12T18:00:46+00:00">February 12, 2016</time><time class="updated" datetime="2016-02-12T13:56:51+00:00">February 12, 2016</time></a></span><span class="byline"><span class="author vcard"><a class="url fn n" href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/author/tonysreadinglist/">Tony</a></span></span><span class="comments-link"><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2016/02/12/the-sound-of-waves-by-yukio-mishima-review/#comments">11 Comments</a></span>			</div><!-- .entry-meta -->
		
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	
		<div class="entry-content">
			<p><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.files.wordpress.com/2016/02/img_5390.jpg" rel="attachment wp-att-5241"><img class="size-medium wp-image-5241 alignleft" src="https://tonysreadinglist.files.wordpress.com/2016/02/img_5390.jpg?w=300&#038;h=225" alt="IMG_5390" width="300" height="225" /></a>One of the things I like about my <strong>January in Japan</strong> reading (extended into February&#8230;) is the opportunity to get to books which have been languishing on the shelves for a while, and today&#8217;s choice has been kicking around for far too long.  Over the first few years of the blog, I read several works by <strong><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/category/yukio-mishima/">Yukio Mishima</a></strong> (including all four parts of <em><strong><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/category/the-sea-of-fertility-tetralogy/">The Sea of Fertility</a></strong></em> series), which makes it surprising that I never quite found the time to get around to the last one of his I bought.  On that note, then, it&#8217;s time to head off to Japan&#8217;s Pacific coast for a short island holiday, and a meeting with a couple of star-crossed lovers &#8211; I wonder if it&#8217;ll end well&#8230;</p>
<p>*****<br />
<em><strong>The Sound of Waves</strong></em> (translated by <strong>Meredith Weatherby</strong>, published by <strong>Vintage Books</strong>) takes us to the small island of Uta-jima, just off the Japanese mainland.  The hero of the tale is Shinji who, after the death of his fisherman father, has become the breadwinner for his family (his mother and younger brother, Hiroshi).  One day, while walking along the beach, he sees an unfamiliar face, a rarity on the small island:</p>
<p><em><strong>The boy could not remember ever having seen this girl before.  There should not have been a single face on Uta-jima that he could not recognize.  At first glance he took her for an outsider.  But still, the girl&#8217;s dress was not that of outsiders.  Only in the way she stood apart, gazing at the sea, did she differ from the vivacious island girls.</strong></em><br />
<strong>p.8 (Vintage Classics, 2000)</strong></p>
<p>While it&#8217;s not love at first sight, there&#8217;s certainly mutual attraction, and Shinji tries to find out more about her.</p>
<p>It turns out that the girl&#8217;s name is Hatsue, and she&#8217;s the daughter of the wealthy Terukichi Miyata; having been adopted out, she has recently been brought back to the island after the untimely death of Miyata&#8217;s only son.  Shinji naturally falls for the beautiful young woman, but with the father wanting a well-off son-in-law to marry Hatsue and be adopted into the family, it&#8217;s unlikely that the young fisherman will be able to impress him.  Still, that&#8217;s not going to put off a determined suitor, and soon the whole island is aware of the burgeoning romance&#8230;</p>
<p>If you&#8217;ve read a lot of Mishima&#8217;s work, <em>The Sound of Waves</em> may come as a surprise.  It&#8217;s a gentle-paced, soothing romance, a book I&#8217;d best describe as very unMishima-like &#8211; in fact, one Twitter comment (I will protect the user&#8217;s anonymity&#8230;) even called this his dullest book.  Which seems a little unfair to me.  It&#8217;s true that there&#8217;s no real drama, and a distinct lack of blood, but if you scratch the surface, there&#8217;s a lot to like about the book.</p>
<p>The main focus is, of course, on the romance, and we follow the young lovers along their path to happiness, witnessing their coincidental meetings, and the significance of the dropped envelope full of Shinji&#8217;s wages.  Eventually, the innocent flirtation leads to an unexpected encounter on a dark and stormy night, and a heated moment of temptation.  However, this is no steamy story; both products of a deeply traditional island culture, the pair are determined to go about things the right way lest they anger the gods.</p>
<p>There are complications, naturally (it wouldn&#8217;t be much of a novel, otherwise), and one of these is the obvious gulf in status between them, one shown in many different ways:</p>
<p><em><strong>Shinji looked back.  The girl was standing there, laughing.</strong></em><br />
<em><strong>&#8220;What is it?&#8221; he asked.</strong></em><br />
<em><strong>&#8220;I&#8217;m dark too, but you &#8211; you&#8217;re practically black.&#8221;</strong></em><br />
<em><strong>&#8220;What?&#8221;</strong></em><br />
<em><strong>&#8220;You&#8217;ve really been burnt by the sun, you have.&#8221; </strong></em><strong>(p.31)</strong></p>
<p>Whether it&#8217;s Shinji&#8217;s family background, appearance or lack of money, Hatsue&#8217;s father has no interest in allowing his daughter to follow her heart, instead opting for rich kid Yasuo Kawamoto.  Matters aren&#8217;t helped when Chiyoko, another returning local whose feelings for Shinji cripple her with jealousy, spreads rumours which cause trouble for the young lovers.  Throughout, there&#8217;s a sense that a closed community like Uta-jima needs order &#8211; and that people shouldn&#8217;t get above their station.</p>
<p>If that was all there was to <em>The Sound of Waves</em>, then I&#8217;d agree that it wouldn&#8217;t make for much, but there is far more than this to enjoy.  For one thing, a major part of the appeal is the setting and the writer&#8217;s description of the island and the connection the locals have with it:</p>
<p><em><strong>The boy felt a consummate accord between himself and this opulence of nature that surrounded him.  He inhaled deeply, and it was as though a part of the unseen something that constitutes nature had permeated the core of his being.  He heard the sound of the waves striking the shore, and it was as though the surging of his young blood was keeping time with the movement of the sea&#8217;s great tides.  It was doubtless because nature itself satisfied his need that Shinji felt no particular lack of music in his everyday life. </strong></em><strong>(pp.44/5)</strong></p>
<p>As the novel progresses, the annual cycle of the island is almost as prominent as the plot.  There are the women divers with their different tasks, the seasonal fishing, the driving rain and salt air.  On Uta-jima, the people are still controlled by the passing of time, not to mention the whims of the weather gods&#8230;</p>
<p>Mishima also creates a sense of a tipping point.  The novel is set a few years after the end of World War Two, and while it&#8217;s often easy to forget this, relaxing in a timeless idyll, we&#8217;re eventually dragged back into the present by mentions of electricity, or the trams and movies Hiroshi sees on his trip to Kyoto.  Surprisingly, Mishima is actually fairly positive about progress, in his emphasis on modernity firmly behind Shinji&#8217;s battles against old money and traditional attitudes, with the sympathy of the reader also with the young man.</p>
<p>Of course, despite this optimism for the future, with Mishima there&#8217;s always a sense of looking longingly over your shoulder at the past.  The islanders are often described as being uninterested in what&#8217;s happening on the mainland, and many of the negative qualities displayed in the book are shown to have come from elsewhere (e.g. Chiyoko&#8217;s jealousy, Yasuo&#8217;s laziness &#8211; and his leather jacket!).  Despite the advances in some areas, Uta-jima is painted to be an unspoiled idyll, with a population of noble and understanding people &#8211; which can be a little unrealistic at times <span class='wp-smiley wp-emoji wp-emoji-wink' title=';)'>;)</span></p>
<p>I&#8217;d agree that <em>The Sound of Waves</em> is not one of Mishima&#8217;s best books, but it&#8217;s still one I enjoyed, a nice look at traditional island culture in Japan.  Yes, it&#8217;s not really much more than <em><strong>Romeo and Juliet</strong></em> on a small island, but there&#8217;s nothing wrong with that.  If you&#8217;re fed up of complex, depressing reads and are looking for something a little lighter, this might make a nice change <span class='wp-smiley wp-emoji wp-emoji-smile' title=':)'>:)</span></p>
					</div><!-- .entry-content -->

	
	<footer class="entry-meta entry-footer">
		<span class="cat-links"><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/category/january-in-japan/" rel="category tag">January in Japan</a>, <a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/category/japan/" rel="category tag">Japan</a>, <a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/category/review/" rel="category tag">Review</a>, <a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/category/yukio-mishima/" rel="category tag">Yukio Mishima</a></span>	</footer><!-- .entry-footer -->
</article><!-- #post-5229 -->
			
				
<article id="post-5263" class="post-5263 post type-post status-publish format-standard hentry category-han-kang category-portobello-books category-review category-south-korea">
	<header class="entry-header">

		<h2 class="entry-title"><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2016/02/10/human-acts-by-han-kang-review/" rel="bookmark">&#8216;Human Acts&#8217; by Han Kang&nbsp;(Review)</a></h2>
					<div class="entry-meta">
				<span class="posted-on"><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2016/02/10/human-acts-by-han-kang-review/" rel="bookmark"><time class="entry-date published" datetime="2016-02-10T18:00:32+00:00">February 10, 2016</time><time class="updated" datetime="2016-02-16T13:17:44+00:00">February 16, 2016</time></a></span><span class="byline"><span class="author vcard"><a class="url fn n" href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/author/tonysreadinglist/">Tony</a></span></span><span class="comments-link"><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2016/02/10/human-acts-by-han-kang-review/#comments">13 Comments</a></span>			</div><!-- .entry-meta -->
		
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	
		<div class="entry-content">
			<p><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.files.wordpress.com/2016/02/img_5393.jpg" rel="attachment wp-att-5266"><img class="size-medium wp-image-5266 alignleft" src="https://tonysreadinglist.files.wordpress.com/2016/02/img_5393.jpg?w=300&#038;h=225" alt="IMG_5393" width="300" height="225" /></a>At the beginning of 2015, <strong><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/category/han-kang/">Han Kang</a></strong> was a fairly unknown Korean writer, with her first translation into English about to be published. Moving forward a year, <strong><em><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2015/02/05/the-vegetarian-by-han-kang-review/">The Vegetarian</a></em></strong> is a huge success, appearing on many end-of-year lists, and with an American release imminent, the exposure is unlikely to go away soon (see, for example, <a href="http://www.theguardian.com/books/2016/feb/05/han-kang-interview-writing-massacre?CMP=share_btn_tw">this recent interview</a> in <em><strong>The Guardian</strong></em>). In fact, her star is likely to rise further with the release of her follow-up novel in the UK.  Just as well-written, and fascinating, as her first work in English, this one is slightly less whimsical and allegorical than its predecessor.  Sadly, it is also far more disturbing, a book which looks at human nature and what happens when it is put to the test.</p>
<p>*****<br />
<strong><em><a href="http://portobellobooks.com/human-acts">Human Acts</a></em></strong> (translated by <strong>Deborah Smith</strong>, review copy courtesy of <strong><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/category/portobello-books/">Portobello Books</a></strong>) takes us to the Korean city of Gwangju in May 1980 where, after a bloody government crackdown on a mass demonstration, the army has temporarily left the city. As civilian militias led by students and schoolboys draw up futile plans to defend their city from more carnage, people roam the streets looking for the missing and the dead. Dong-ho, a middle-school student, is searching for his friend Jeong-dae, having been unable to retrieve his friend’s body after he was gunned down in broad daylight. Despite his tender years, he ends up helping to organise the dead, taking down details and attaching numbers to the unidentified victims of the military’s cruelty.</p>
<p>With the day drawing to a close, and the army’s return imminent, all those who care for him (high-school girl Eun-sook, university student Seon-ju, militia organiser Jin-su and Dong-ho’s poor mother) urge the young boy to go home before it is too late. Sadly, none of them succeed in making him leave, a decision which will see him cut down before his life has really begun. But that is merely one part of the story. As the novel progresses, we are told other sides of the tale, with Dong-ho’s (and Gwangju’s) fate elaborated upon by those who survived the horror &#8211; and some who didn’t…</p>
<p>The Gwangju Uprising (or Massacre, depending on your viewpoint) is an incredible story, and one I had never even heard of a year or so ago, a fact I find hard to believe now. The incident is still controversial today, a modern, developed state at war with one of its own cities, ready to wipe it out rather than give in to what the government saw as leftist sedition. In basing her novel here, Han, a native of Gwangju who moved to Seoul as a child, uses the incident as the background to an astonishing, and confronting, introduction to the best and worst human nature has to offer. A tale in seven parts, it examines how people react in stressful times, and the effects these events have on their future.</p>
<p>In her comments on the book, Han is firm in her belief that the novel, while using Gwangju as a base, is just as much about the way people in general behave, hence the choice of the title <em>Human Acts</em> for the translation. There are several examples of focusing on the human in us: Jeong-dae’s memories of his sister; the childhood games he played with Dong-ho; the heroism of students and workers in the city; and the way the pro-democracy movement lifted individuals above themselves:</p>
<p><strong><em>Those snapshot moments, when it seemed we’d all performed the miracle of stepping outside the shell of our own selves, one person’s tender skin coming into grazed contact with another, felt as though they were rethreading the sinews of that world heart, patching up the fissures from which blood had flowed, making it beat again. </em></strong><strong>(p.122)</strong></p>
<p>In these heady moments, there is a feeling that the whole can truly be greater than the sum of its parts, a crowd of people working together to achieve great things.</p>
<p>Sadly, though, this is just as true for the darker side of life, and <em>Human Acts</em> shows us all too well how people’s behaviour can also sicken and horrify. As people in Gwangju try to help the wounded, they are cut down by snipers. Children surrendering to government troops are gunned down in cold blood. Women and children are beaten mercilessly by soldiers, whose orders originate from the very highest levels of power:</p>
<p><strong><em>You never forgot the face of the plain-clothes policeman who had stamped on you. You never forgot that the government actively trained and supported the strike-breakers, that at the peak of this pyramid of violence stood President Park Chung-hee himself, an army general who has seized power through a military coup.</em></strong><strong> (p.165)</strong></p>
<p>The casual beatings of women, using the excuse of their being red sympathisers to justify the behaviour, is unheard of in Korean culture. The women on the receiving end are almost more shocked by the assault on their status as young women as by the beatings themselves.</p>
<p>Perhaps the most important aspect of <em>Human Acts</em>, though, is the aftermath of the intervention. After the initial scene setting up the return of the military to the city, and the poignant second section in which the callous treatment of the victim’s corpses is described by the soul of one of the dead, Han slowly moves us forward in time, inching year by year into the future, one which, for many of the characters, holds little hope of closure. We meet Eun-sook five years on, recovering from seven brutal slaps to the face for withholding information she doesn’t have. Another five years sees a prisoner telling the story of his torture in prison and his relationship with the unfortunate Jin-su. Fast forward to 2002, and Seon-ju has her turn at remembering the past, now isolated (mainly by choice) and documenting the slow deaths of others let down by Korean society. Finally, we arrive in 2010, where Dong-ho’s mother is still haunted by the past, never forgetting, never forgiving.</p>
<p>These people, just as much as those who fell in the streets in May 1980, are victims of the Gwangju massacre, shattered and broken, their lives destroyed. Emotionally they are withdrawn, their failed relationships the result of being unable to bear intimacy after the soldiers’ attacks on their bodies. Dreams of improving their lives by study are just that – with the universities controlled by the regime, many have no choice but to stay away. Some attempt to bury themselves in work, but these are jobs with no future. Blacklisted by the government, they are stuck in dead-end jobs, hounded from one place to the next. Their lives are connected by their shared experiences and trauma, yet their pain leaves them unable to touch each other, a situation foreshadowed, ironically enough, in the chapter with the souls. Each senses the other souls around them, unable to connect…</p>
<p>As was the case with <em>The Vegetarian</em>, Deborah Smith has done an excellent job of sustaining the writer’s slightly withdrawn approach to an emotional subject, showing again that having a sympathetic translator with a skill for writing in English is more important than finding one who knows the source culture intimately. Smith also contributes a short introduction detailing the background and importance of the events of May 1980 as well as commenting on some of the translation issues. On rereading the book, I was able to pay closer attention to the translator&#8217;s treatment of the part featuring Dong-ho’s mother, in which the thick Gwangju dialect is rendered in a light, cheery Northern English accent (as I read it to myself, memories of my years living in Leeds helped me find her voice&#8230;). The obvious sympathy Smith has with Han and her story has helped in the development of the English version.</p>
<p>Another small translation issue involves the title, and Smith again discusses this in <a href="http://www.asymptotejournal.com/criticism/han-kang-human-acts/">a short essay available at <strong><em>Asymptote</em></strong></a>.  The original Korean title was <strong><em>Sonyeon-i Onda</em></strong>, which could be literally translated as ‘the young boy is coming’, a clumsy and unfortunate expression in English. After rejecting several variations on this, the team working on the translation eventually settled on <em>Human Acts</em>, a title which brings out the importance of the human nature of the novel. However, the original title is also important. Over the course of the survivors’ accounts, Dong-ho gradually comes into focus, approaching us through the grief of the others. In the performance of a play that Eun-sook helped to publish, she hears the following lines:</p>
<p><em> </em><strong><em>After you died I could not hold a funeral,<br />
And so my life became a funeral </em></strong><strong>(p.105)</strong></p>
<p>Immediately she thinks of Dong-ho, and this appears to be the motto of all those who feel guilt at the boy’s death.</p>
<p>Han is not the only writer, or even the first, to tackle the theme of Gwangju. <strong><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/category/choe-yun/">Ch’oe Yun’s</a></strong> novella <strong><em><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2014/08/21/there-a-petal-silently-falls-by-choe-yun-review/">There a Petal Silently Falls</a></em></strong> takes a similar polyphonic approach, examining the fate of an unnamed girl from several viewpoints. <strong><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/category/hwang-sok-yong/">Hwang Sok-yong’s</a></strong> lengthy novel <strong><em><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2016/01/04/the-old-garden-by-hwang-sok-yong-review/">The Old Garden</a></em></strong> focuses instead on a later period, with descriptions of prison life and the long-term effects on survivors. However, Han ties all these themes together, the seven parts of the novel working as a whole to tell the story of the massacre. They also slowly, piece by piece, complete the picture of Dong-ho (which, appropriately, is represented by the school photo the writer sees in the final section), while gradually constructing a fuller picture of the wider societal impacts.</p>
<p>For Han, <em>Human Acts</em> is a rather personal work, and in the final part of the story, in which a writer returns to Gwangju, the line between fiction and real life is, at best, slightly blurred. In the best traditions of Korean (and Japanese) fiction, the writer expresses her own feelings through a literary alter-ego, one only slightly removed from the real thing. This return to Gwangju, taking place in 2013, is perhaps prompted by the recent rise to power of the new Korean President Park Geun-hye, the daughter of the dictator who initiated the series of crack-downs culminating in the Gwangju uprising.</p>
<p>On the writer’s return, Gwangju is a very different city; many of the buildings are gone, the bullet holes have mostly disappeared, crime scenes have been painted over. However, the memories, the memories remain:</p>
<p><strong><em>There were soldiers who were especially cruel.<br />
When I first started poring over the documents, what had proved most incomprehensible was that this bloodshed had been committed again and again, and with no attempt to bring the perpetrators before the authorities. Acts of violence committed in broad daylight, without hesitation and without regret. Commanding officers who would have encouraged, no even demanded such displays of brutality.</em></strong><strong> (p.214)</strong></p>
<p>These wounds are still fresh in the minds and hearts of the people of Gwanju, and Park’s election is tantamount to ripping the plasters off those wounds, callously poking cruel fingers between the stitches. Seen in that light, then, Han’s book is less an attempt to understand the past than a work of documentation. Whatever happens, however successful Korea becomes, the voice of the survivors of Gwangju will always be heard &#8211; we will not forget…</p>
					</div><!-- .entry-content -->

	
	<footer class="entry-meta entry-footer">
		<span class="cat-links"><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/category/han-kang/" rel="category tag">Han Kang</a>, <a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/category/portobello-books/" rel="category tag">Portobello Books</a>, <a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/category/review/" rel="category tag">Review</a>, <a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/category/south-korea/" rel="category tag">South Korea</a></span>	</footer><!-- .entry-footer -->
</article><!-- #post-5263 -->
			
				
<article id="post-5227" class="post-5227 post type-post status-publish format-standard hentry category-andres-barba category-hispabooks category-luisge-martin category-review category-spain">
	<header class="entry-header">

		<h2 class="entry-title"><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2016/02/09/august-october-by-andres-barba-the-same-city-by-luisge-martin-review/" rel="bookmark">&#8216;August, October&#8217; by Andrés Barba / &#8216;The Same City&#8217; by Luisgé Martín&nbsp;(Review)</a></h2>
					<div class="entry-meta">
				<span class="posted-on"><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2016/02/09/august-october-by-andres-barba-the-same-city-by-luisge-martin-review/" rel="bookmark"><time class="entry-date published" datetime="2016-02-09T06:00:41+00:00">February 9, 2016</time><time class="updated" datetime="2016-02-08T17:23:57+00:00">February 8, 2016</time></a></span><span class="byline"><span class="author vcard"><a class="url fn n" href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/author/tonysreadinglist/">Tony</a></span></span><span class="comments-link"><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2016/02/09/august-october-by-andres-barba-the-same-city-by-luisge-martin-review/#comments">2 Comments</a></span>			</div><!-- .entry-meta -->
		
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	
		<div class="entry-content">
			<p><strong><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.files.wordpress.com/2016/02/img_5387.jpg" rel="attachment wp-att-5233"><img class="size-medium wp-image-5233 alignleft" src="https://tonysreadinglist.files.wordpress.com/2016/02/img_5387.jpg?w=300&#038;h=225" alt="IMG_5387" width="300" height="225" /></a><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/category/hispabooks/">Hispabooks</a></strong>, a Madrid-based press specialising in translations of literary fiction from the four official languages of Spain, has been around for a few years now and has introduced several interesting Spanish writers to Anglophone readers.  However, as important as breadth is, depth is also necessary, which is why I was pleased to see two writers whose work I enjoyed, <strong><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/category/andres-barba/">Andrés Barba</a></strong> and <strong><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/category/luisge-martin/">Luisgé Martín</a></strong> featured for a second time in the Hispabooks catalogue.  Having received review copies of both, I thought it would be nice to look at them together, even if they&#8217;re (longish) novellas with very different settings and themes &#8211; let&#8217;s see which one I liked better <span class='wp-smiley wp-emoji wp-emoji-smile' title=':)'>:)</span></p>
<p>*****<br />
<strong>Luisgé Martín&#8217;s <em><a href="http://hispabooks.com/home/25-the-same-city.html">The Same City</a></em></strong> (translated by <strong>Tomasz Dukanovich</strong>) has the writer recounting the story of Brandon Moy, an American poet he used to know.  A man of the world, the American has lived in several countries, done a lot of crazy things and slept with dozens of women along the way &#8211; which makes it difficult for the writer to believe Moy when he adds one more detail to the story.  Apparently, the catalyst for all this was the attacks on the World Trade Center &#8211; when Moy (in truth a lawyer) realised that everyone thought he was at work in one of the buildings, he decided to take the opportunity to run away in search of an elusive freedom he thought had gone forever.</p>
<p>With his last doubts decided by the failure of the call to his wife to go through, Moy leaves New<a href="https://tonysreadinglist.files.wordpress.com/2016/02/img_5388.jpg" rel="attachment wp-att-5234"><img class="size-medium wp-image-5234 alignright" src="https://tonysreadinglist.files.wordpress.com/2016/02/img_5388.jpg?w=225&#038;h=300" alt="IMG_5388" width="225" height="300" /></a> York to start a new life, deciding to pursue all the interests he was forced to give up once his wife, family and work came along.  Starting from the bottom, he manages to create a different life for himself from scratch, living in a way he could previously only have dreamed of.  However, a sense of doubt lingers, eventually clouding over his happiness, a feeling which is crystallised in a poem by Constantine Cavafy which he is introduced to by one of new lovers:</p>
<p><em><strong>You won&#8217;t find a new country, won&#8217;t find another shore.</strong></em><br />
<em><strong>This city will always pursue you.</strong></em><br />
<strong>p.79 (Hispabooks, 2015)</strong></p>
<p>Sadly, in his pursuit of lost ambitions, the realisation comes too late; the truth is that he was probably happy where he was.</p>
<p><em>The Same City</em> is an interesting slant on the mid-life crisis (indeed this idea crops up on the first page), one where the reader vicariously experiences the thrill of leaving everything behind.  The writer telling the story is, like the reader, half in awe of Moy and half shocked by his ability to shake off the dust of his old life, but Martín&#8217;s account of the American&#8217;s experiences, including how he begins to lie, cheat, steal and sleep around, pulls no punches.  Gradually, we see that no matter how free Moy feels, in truth, the grass is rarely greener elsewhere, no matter how jaded you&#8217;ve become.  Wherever you run to in search of new experiences, you&#8217;ll end up in the same city you left.</p>
<p>There&#8217;s a lot to like about <em>The Same City</em>, but I&#8217;d have to say that I preferred his last book in English, <em><strong><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2015/07/16/woman-in-darkness-by-luisge-martin-review/">Woman in Darkness</a></strong></em>, much more.  The main issue I had with it was that it dragged a little in places, and while the repetitive nature of Moy&#8217;s actions may have been intended to emphasise how he repeatedly becomes trapped by his life, it also had me wanting to skip ahead at times.  I think this is because <em>The Same City</em> is a short story which has been extended beyond its limits; I suspect that the same tale could have been told, and just as well, in thirty to forty pages.  It&#8217;s certainly interesting, but you do wonder if it needs to be as long as it is.</p>
<p>*****<br />
<a href="https://tonysreadinglist.files.wordpress.com/2016/02/img_5389.jpg" rel="attachment wp-att-5235"><img class="size-medium wp-image-5235 alignleft" src="https://tonysreadinglist.files.wordpress.com/2016/02/img_5389.jpg?w=225&#038;h=300" alt="IMG_5389" width="225" height="300" /></a>However, that&#8217;s certainly not the case for the second of today&#8217;s books, <strong>Andrés Barba&#8217;s <em><a href="http://hispabooks.com/home/26-august-october.html">August, October</a></em></strong> (translated by <strong>Lisa Dillman</strong>).  <em><strong><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2014/12/16/rain-over-madrid-by-andres-barba-review/">Rain Over Madrid</a></strong></em> featured four extended stories, and this second work in English pushes the range a little further, with a two-part novella running to almost 150 pages.  The story looks at a fourteen-year-old Spanish boy on the annual family holiday to the coastal town where his aunt lives.  Where previous years were full of fun and sun, puberty has wrought changes upon Tomás, and this August is to be very different from those of the past.</p>
<p><em>August, October</em> is a book I enjoyed greatly, recognisably by the writer of <em>Rain Over Madrid</em>, but with a slightly harder, grittier feel.  Tomás has entered a difficult period in his life, and everything seems old and faded, leaving him empty and unable to enjoy himself as he used to.  Even the annual visit to the fair has lost its appeal:</p>
<p><em><strong>The first fair, though, the one from the early days, seemed to have been switched off, and it was no longer a luminous highway but a thinly lit stream, slightly asphyxiating &#8211; the charcoal-grilled seafood had a burned smell, and its thick smoke was off-putting&#8230;</strong></em><br />
<strong>p.53 (Hispabooks, 2015)</strong></p>
<p>When you add to this teenage angst the discovery that his aunt is terminally ill, it&#8217;s no wonder that he is about to go off the rails.</p>
<p>The twist in the tale comes when he falls in with a group of local youths, boys he&#8217;d never normally hang around with, and comes into the orbit of their female equivalents, more sexually aware and forthright than the girls Tomás is accustomed to.  Mix in some drink, drugs and sexual frustration, and the scene is set for a dramatic end to his holiday, a rather disturbing encounter which leaves him guilt-ridden and desperate.  Haunted by the memory of what happened even back in the comfort of his own home, it&#8217;s inevitable that he&#8217;ll return to the coast to try to make things right.</p>
<p>Portraying Tomás in a sympathetic manner is no easy task, but Barba manages it, thanks in part in this version to Dillman, whose translation reads beautifully, capturing the swing between the melancholy lows and the tension-filled highs of the teenager&#8217;s experiences.  In places, <em>August, October</em> even reminded me a little of <a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2010/03/10/review-post-11-from-the-comfortably-sublime-to-the-absurdly-ridiculous/"><strong>Camus&#8217;</strong> <em><strong>The Stranger</strong></em></a>; quite apart from the nihilistic air pervading many parts of the novel, there&#8217;s a tense first encounter with the local youths on the beach, a slow walk under a burning sun.  Throw in the rather abrupt ending to the first part of the story, and you could almost be in North Africa&#8230;</p>
<p>However, unlike Camus, Barba is here to redeem his &#8216;hero&#8217;, not bury him, and the second part of <em>August, October</em> moves towards a happier climax.  Tomás&#8217; quest for absolution turns into a voyage of discovery as he learns a little more about himself and questions what he wants from life.  The themes, and the narrative arc, make this a <em>Bildungsroman</em>, even verging on YA at times, but there&#8217;s a lot more to the story than this.  Where <em>The Same City</em> unfortunately outstayed its welcome a little, <em>August, October</em> ends at just the right spot, allowing the reader to imagine its hero at a crossroads.  Whichever path he takes, though, we sense that he&#8217;ll be moving in the right direction.</p>
					</div><!-- .entry-content -->

	
	<footer class="entry-meta entry-footer">
		<span class="cat-links"><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/category/andres-barba/" rel="category tag">Andrés Barba</a>, <a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/category/hispabooks/" rel="category tag">Hispabooks</a>, <a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/category/luisge-martin/" rel="category tag">Luisgé Martín</a>, <a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/category/review/" rel="category tag">Review</a>, <a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/category/spain/" rel="category tag">Spain</a></span>	</footer><!-- .entry-footer -->
</article><!-- #post-5227 -->
			
				
<article id="post-5180" class="post-5180 post type-post status-publish format-standard hentry category-man-booker-international-prize-2016">
	<header class="entry-header">

		<h2 class="entry-title"><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2016/02/07/man-booker-international-prize-2016-predictions/" rel="bookmark">Man Booker International Prize 2016 &#8211;&nbsp;Predictions</a></h2>
					<div class="entry-meta">
				<span class="posted-on"><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2016/02/07/man-booker-international-prize-2016-predictions/" rel="bookmark"><time class="entry-date published" datetime="2016-02-07T18:00:25+00:00">February 7, 2016</time><time class="updated" datetime="2016-02-08T11:13:32+00:00">February 8, 2016</time></a></span><span class="byline"><span class="author vcard"><a class="url fn n" href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/author/tonysreadinglist/">Tony</a></span></span><span class="comments-link"><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2016/02/07/man-booker-international-prize-2016-predictions/#comments">11 Comments</a></span>			</div><!-- .entry-meta -->
		
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	
		<div class="entry-content">
			<p><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.files.wordpress.com/2016/02/mbip-2016.jpg" rel="attachment wp-att-5184"><img class="size-full wp-image-5184 alignleft" src="https://tonysreadinglist.files.wordpress.com/2016/02/mbip-2016.jpg?w=636" alt="MBIP 2016"   /></a>The <strong>Independent Foreign Fiction Prize</strong> is dead &#8211; long live the <strong>Man Booker International Prize</strong>!  Well, that&#8217;s what the organisers of the new prize would have us think, anyway.  Those of us who followed the old prize might not be quite so gung-ho about the change, but it&#8217;s almost time to see how the brave new world of British literary translation prizes unfolds.  The schedule for the 2016 edition is as follows:</p>
<p style="text-align:center;"><strong>10 March 2016 &#8211; Longlist Announcement</strong><br />
<strong> 14 April 2016 &#8211; Shortlist Announcement</strong><br />
<strong> 16 May 2016 &#8211; Winner Announcement</strong></p>
<p>While it may be the start of a new era, <strong>Boyd Tonkin</strong> is still on board as one of the judges, and a selection of the usual blogging suspects will also be back to shadow the prize and keep the &#8216;real&#8217; judges honest (more about that in the weeks to come).  But just what will we be reading?  Well, today I&#8217;ll share a few thoughts on what might be on the list next month (all links to my reviews, where applicable), and as you&#8217;ll quickly notice, I&#8217;ve come up with a rather long list of possibles.  It seems to have been a pretty good year in the world of fiction in translation&#8230;<br />
<strong>(Note: post amended 8/2/16 &#8211; I now realise that not all Scribe Australia titles appear through Scribe UK&#8230;)</strong></p>
<p>*****<br />
<a href="https://tonysreadinglist.files.wordpress.com/2015/02/img_5156.jpg" rel="attachment wp-att-3186"><img class="size-medium wp-image-3186 alignright" src="https://tonysreadinglist.files.wordpress.com/2015/02/img_5156.jpg?w=225&#038;h=300" alt="'The Vegetarian' by Han Kang (Review)" width="225" height="300" /></a>Where literary prizes are concerned, any talk of certainties is tantamount to tempting fate, yet there are some books I&#8217;d be surprised not to see on the list.  <strong>Han Kang&#8217;s <em><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2015/02/05/the-vegetarian-by-han-kang-review/">The Vegetarian</a></em></strong> (translated by <strong>Deborah Smith</strong>) has received huge amounts of praise since its release, and the amount of publicity given to <strong>Kamel Daoud&#8217;s <em><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2015/06/08/the-mersault-investigation-by-kamel-daoud-review/">The Meursault Investigation</a></em></strong> (tr. <strong>John Cullen</strong>) and <strong>Fiston Mwanza Mujila&#8217;s <em><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2015/09/15/tram-83-by-fiston-mwanza-mujila-review/">Tram 83</a></em></strong> (tr. <strong>Roland Glasser</strong>) makes me think they&#8217;ll be included.  Another probable contender is <em><strong><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2014/10/30/seiobo-there-below-by-laszlo-krasznahorkai-review/">Seiobo There Below</a></strong></em> (tr. <strong>Ottilie Mulzet</strong>) as <strong>László Krasznahorkai&#8217;s</strong> novel has already tasted success in the shape of the <strong>Best Translated Book Award</strong> for its US edition.</p>
<p><em><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.files.wordpress.com/2015/06/img_5236.jpg" rel="attachment wp-att-3798"><img class="size-medium wp-image-3798 alignleft" src="https://tonysreadinglist.files.wordpress.com/2015/06/img_5236.jpg?w=300&#038;h=225" alt="IMG_5236" width="300" height="225" /></a>Seiobo&#8230;</em> isn&#8217;t the only book which crossed the pond last year, and several others have a good chance of making the cut.  <em><strong><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2015/04/28/in-the-night-of-time-by-antonio-munoz-molina-review/">In the Night of Time</a></strong></em> (tr. <strong>Edith Grossman</strong>), <strong>Antonio Muñoz Molina&#8217;s</strong> expansive novel set during the Spanish Civil War, has impressed those who have chosen to tackle it, and while <strong><span class="st">Mathias Énard</span>&#8216;s</strong> <em><strong><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2015/05/19/street-of-thieves-by-mathias-enard-review/">Street of Thieves</a></strong></em> (tr. <strong>Charlotte Mandell</strong>) didn&#8217;t quite live up to the sublime <em><strong><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2014/12/23/zone-by-mathias-enard-review/">Zone</a></strong></em>, it&#8217;s still an excellent read.  Then there&#8217;s <strong>Alejandro Zambra&#8217;s</strong> short-story collection <em><strong><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2015/06/15/my-documents-by-alejandro-zambra-review/">My Documents</a></strong></em> (tr. <strong>Megan McDowell</strong>), a book which had me hunting down all of the Chilean writer&#8217;s work translated so far <span class='wp-smiley wp-emoji wp-emoji-smile' title=':)'>:)</span></p>
<p>Last year I reviewed several books for <strong>Scribe Publications</strong>, an Australian press with a UK <a href="https://tonysreadinglist.files.wordpress.com/2015/06/img_5234.jpg" rel="attachment wp-att-3764"><img class="size-medium wp-image-3764 alignright" src="https://tonysreadinglist.files.wordpress.com/2015/06/img_5234.jpg?w=300&#038;h=225" alt="IMG_5234" width="300" height="225" /></a>presence, and I can see a few with a chance of being longlisted (although not all of them were published by Scribe in the UK!).  <strong>Hwang Sok-yong&#8217;s <em><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2015/06/01/princess-bari-by-hwang-sok-yong-review/">Princess Bari</a></em></strong> (tr. <strong>Sora Kim-Russell, published by Periscope Books</strong>) would add some much-needed geographical variety to the list, as would Brazilian writer <strong>Tatiana Salem Levy&#8217;s <em><a href="http://shinynewbooks.co.uk/fiction-issue-6/new-the-house-in-smyrna-by-tatiana-salem-levy/">The House in Smyrna</a></em></strong> (tr. <strong>Alison Entrekin</strong>).  A more likely candidate, though, might be <strong>Tommy Wieringa</strong>, and his most recent novel, <em><strong><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2015/03/31/these-are-the-names-by-tommy-wieringa-review/">These are the Names</a></strong></em> (tr. <strong>Sam Garrett</strong>), is an outside chance.  And let&#8217;s not forget former IFFP winner <strong>Gerbrand Bakker</strong>, whose novel <em><strong><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2015/07/21/june-by-gerbrand-bakker-review/">June</a></strong></em> (tr. <strong>David Colmer, released by Harvill Secker in the UK</strong>), while not one of my favourites last year, may well catch the eye of the judges.</p>
<p><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.files.wordpress.com/2015/02/img_5168.jpg" rel="attachment wp-att-3319"><img class="size-medium wp-image-3319 alignleft" src="https://tonysreadinglist.files.wordpress.com/2015/02/img_5168.jpg?w=300&#038;h=225" alt="IMG_5168" width="300" height="225" /></a>That&#8217;s already a solid set of contenders, but there are a lot more books with a chance of being chosen for the longlist.  <strong>Jón Kalman Stefánsson&#8217;s <em><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2013/09/16/the-sorrow-of-angels-by-jon-kalman-stefansson-review/">The Sorrow of Angels</a></em></strong> took out our Shadow IFFP in 2014, and <em><strong><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2015/02/24/the-heart-of-man-by-jon-kalman-stefansson-review/">The Heart of Man</a></strong></em> (tr. <strong>Philip Roughton</strong>), the last book in his trilogy, must be in contention this year.  <strong>Elena Ferrante</strong> hasn&#8217;t had any luck in the UK so far, but the last book in her series of Neapolitan novels, <em><strong><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2015/08/31/the-story-of-the-lost-child-by-elena-ferrante-review/">The Story of the Lost Child</a></strong></em> (tr. <strong>Ann Goldstein</strong>), might see that situation change.  Of course, that other serial offender, <strong>Karl Ove Knausgaard</strong>, has another eligible book, <em><strong><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2015/05/10/dancing-in-the-dark-by-karl-ove-knausgaard-review/">Dancing in the Dark</a></strong></em> (tr. <strong>Don Bartlett</strong>) &#8211; it isn&#8217;t one I&#8217;d pick, but you never know&#8230;</p>
<p>Then, there are some real heavyweight names who can&#8217;t be ignored.  On the back of <strong>Patrick <a href="https://tonysreadinglist.files.wordpress.com/2016/01/img_5384.jpg" rel="attachment wp-att-5167"><img class="size-medium wp-image-5167 alignright" src="https://tonysreadinglist.files.wordpress.com/2016/01/img_5384.jpg?w=225&#038;h=300" alt="IMG_5384" width="225" height="300" /></a>Modiano&#8217;s</strong> Nobel Prize win, any one of the new releases might make the grade (your guess is as good as mine when it comes to which one&#8230;), as could fellow laureate <strong>Kenzaburō Ōe</strong><strong>&#8216;s <em><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2016/01/28/death-by-water-by-kenzaburo-oe-review/">Death by Water</a> </em></strong>(tr. <strong>Deborah Boliver Boehm</strong>) or <strong>Orhan Pamuk&#8217;s <em>A Strangeness in my Mind</em></strong> (tr. <strong>Ekin Oklap</strong>).  Two writers who haven&#8217;t won the Nobel Prize despite being highly fancied are <strong>Haruki Murakami</strong> and <strong>Umberto Eco</strong>, and <em><strong>Wind/Pinball</strong></em>, the new translation (by <strong>Ted Goossen</strong>) of Murakami&#8217;s early novellas, and <em><strong>Numero Zero</strong></em> (tr. <strong>Richard Dixon</strong>) are also possible longlisters.</p>
<p><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.files.wordpress.com/2015/11/img_5347.jpg" rel="attachment wp-att-4760"><img class="size-medium wp-image-4760 alignleft" src="https://tonysreadinglist.files.wordpress.com/2015/11/img_5347.jpg?w=300&#038;h=225" alt="IMG_5347" width="300" height="225" /></a>Wait &#8211; that&#8217;s not all&#8230;  You see, for the 2016 prize there&#8217;s an extended eligibility period, from the 1st of January, 2015, to the 30th of April, 2016 (from 2017, the eligibility period will run from May to April).  This means that several recent releases will be eligible, assuming they were submitted.  The best example of this is <em><strong>Human Acts</strong></em> (tr. Deborah Smith), with Han Kang&#8217;s latest book in English possibly vying for a spot on the longlist with her previous novel.  It also means that the longlist may contain books which haven&#8217;t even been published yet, such as <strong>Judith Hermann&#8217;s <em><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2015/11/23/aller-liebe-anfang-the-start-of-love-by-judith-hermann-review/">Where Love Begins</a></em></strong> (tr. <strong>Margaret Bettauer Dembo</strong>) or <strong>Javier Marías&#8217;</strong> latest novel <em><strong>Thus Bad Begins</strong></em> (tr. <strong>Margaret Jull Costa</strong>)&#8230;</p>
<p>That&#8217;s an impressive pool of books to work from, yet I realise that I haven&#8217;t even considered works from small presses like <strong>Peirene Press</strong>, <strong>And Other Stories</strong>, <strong>Pushkin Press</strong> or <strong>Istros Books</strong>.  Surely one or two of those will have something in contention.  Which all means that there&#8217;s only one thing I can predict with any certainty &#8211; whatever twelve I decide to pick, the real list will undoubtedly be very, very different <span class='wp-smiley wp-emoji wp-emoji-wink' title=';)'>;)</span></p>
					</div><!-- .entry-content -->

	
	<footer class="entry-meta entry-footer">
		<span class="cat-links"><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/category/man-booker-international-prize-2016/" rel="category tag">Man Booker International Prize 2016</a></span>	</footer><!-- .entry-footer -->
</article><!-- #post-5180 -->
			
				
<article id="post-5198" class="post-5198 post type-post status-publish format-standard hentry category-january-in-japan category-japan category-kurodahan-press category-review category-ryu-murakami">
	<header class="entry-header">

		<h2 class="entry-title"><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2016/02/04/tokyo-decadence-by-ryu-murakami-review/" rel="bookmark">&#8216;Tokyo Decadence&#8217; by Ryu Murakami&nbsp;(Review)</a></h2>
					<div class="entry-meta">
				<span class="posted-on"><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2016/02/04/tokyo-decadence-by-ryu-murakami-review/" rel="bookmark"><time class="entry-date published" datetime="2016-02-04T18:00:36+00:00">February 4, 2016</time><time class="updated" datetime="2016-02-03T15:17:13+00:00">February 3, 2016</time></a></span><span class="byline"><span class="author vcard"><a class="url fn n" href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/author/tonysreadinglist/">Tony</a></span></span><span class="comments-link"><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2016/02/04/tokyo-decadence-by-ryu-murakami-review/#comments">4 Comments</a></span>			</div><!-- .entry-meta -->
		
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	
		<div class="entry-content">
			<p><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.files.wordpress.com/2016/02/img_5386.jpg" rel="attachment wp-att-5217"><img class="size-medium wp-image-5217 alignleft" src="https://tonysreadinglist.files.wordpress.com/2016/02/img_5386.jpg?w=300&#038;h=225" alt="IMG_5386" width="300" height="225" /></a>Over the past few years, especially during my January in Japan events, I&#8217;ve read and reviewed several books by small publisher <strong><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/category/kurodahan-press/">Kurodahan Press</a></strong>, who have even been kind enough to offer prizes on occasion.  They&#8217;ve published books by the likes of <a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/category/osamu-dazai/"><strong>Osamu Dazai</strong> </a>and <strong><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/category/teru-miyamoto/">Teru Miyamoto</a></strong>, but in March they have a book coming out from perhaps their biggest-name author yet, one who will be familiar to many of you out there with an interest in J-Lit.  While it might be a stretch to suggest it&#8217;s going to be a best-seller, I do think that it&#8217;s a book with a wider appeal than most &#8211; let me tell you more <span class='wp-smiley wp-emoji wp-emoji-smile' title=':)'>:)</span></p>
<p>*****<br />
<strong><span class="st"><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/category/ryu-murakami/">Ryū Murakami</a> </span></strong><span class="st">has had several novels translated into English (e.g.<em><strong> <a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2015/01/08/almost-transparent-blue-by-ryu-murakami-review/">Almost Transparent Blue</a></strong></em>, <em><strong><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2013/01/28/coin-locker-babies-by-ryu-murakami-review/">Coin Locker Babies</a></strong></em> and <em><strong><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2013/09/01/from-the-fatherland-with-love-by-ryu-murakami-review/">From the Fatherland, With Love</a></strong></em>), but he&#8217;s also written several collections of short stories, of which only a few scattered examples have been sighted in magazines and online journals.  Thankfully, <strong>Ralph McCarthy</strong>, who translated (along with several other Murakami books) most of them,  pitched an idea of a kind of greatest hits collection to Kurodahan, and that&#8217;s how <a href="http://www.kurodahan.com/mt/e/catalog/jp0049cate.html#more"><em><strong>Tokyo Decadence &#8211; 15 Stories by Ryū Murakami</strong></em></a> came about.  The book contains (obviously) fifteen examples of Murakami&#8217;s short fiction, taken from five different books spanning nearly twenty years of his writing career, a selection showcasing all aspects of the writer&#8217;s inimitable style.</span></p>
<p>The first story, <strong>&#8216;Whenever I Sit at a Bar Drinking Like This&#8217;</strong>, eases us gently into the collection, a fun introduction to some of the darker tales to come:</p>
<p><em><strong>Whenever I sit at a bar drinking like this, I&#8217;m reminded what a sacred profession bartending is.  The bartender, with the mirrored shelves of many-colored bottles behind him, moves methodically about in his stained-glass vestibule, like a priest performing a rite.  Pouring the libations into various glasses, he listens with a reverent, sympathetic smile as the congregants recite their woes.</strong></em><br />
<strong>&#8216;Whenever I sit at a Bar Drinking Like This&#8217;, p.3 (Kurodahan Press, 2016)</strong></p>
