<p>Of course, despite this heavenly vision, in Murakami&#8217;s world there are far more sinners than saints, and this clever story of a man desperate for a favour drags us a little down this path.  Needing some help to get out of a sticky situation, he trades promises and favours until luck smiles on him, as it usually does.</p>
<p>This story comes from a collection entitled <em><strong>Run, Takahashi!</strong></em>, where all the stories are linked by this refrain, aimed at Hiroshima Carp baseball player Yoshihiko Takahashi.  In the first story, Takahashi appears in person, but the other stories here have a less personal connection with the player.  Two see their protagonists (the first a famous author, the second a cross-dressing single dad) head off to the stadium while <strong>&#8216;Each Time I Read Your Confession&#8217;</strong> has the baseball action on television in the background &#8211; where Takahashi&#8217;s attempts at stealing bases provide the backdrop to some rather gruesome action&#8230;</p>
<p>While the first four stories are noticeable for their light touch, the second featured collection, <em><strong>Topaz</strong></em>, is far darker.  <em>Tokyo Decadence</em> contains three pieces from this book, all featuring call girls.  <strong>&#8216;Topaz&#8217;</strong> is a melancholy tale detailing a prostitute&#8217;s sad life, taking us through a day in the life of a woman going nowhere, while <strong>&#8216;Lullaby&#8217;</strong> has the main character looking for an old flame and closure after years of regret.</p>
<p><strong>&#8216;Penlight&#8217;</strong> starts off in a far lighter tone, as a woman with an inner voice (called Kiyomi!) blunders chaotically from one sexual encounter to another.  A coincidental meeting with a nice man who wants to help seems to offer a change from the woman&#8217;s depressing life, so why does Kiyomi suddenly fear him so much?  Never fear, gentle reader &#8211; all will be revealed, in an ending with a very R. Murakami twist <span class='wp-smiley wp-emoji wp-emoji-wink' title=';)'>;)</span></p>
<p><span class="st">The next colle</span><span class="st">ction,</span> <em><strong><span class="st">Ryū</span>&#8216;s Cinematheque</strong></em>, is slightly different in tone, with a first-person protagonist reminiscent of the hero of Murakami&#8217;s coming-of-age novel <em><strong><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2013/06/10/sixty-nine-by-ryu-murakami-review/">Sixty-Nine</a></strong></em>.  A young man arrives in Tokyo from the Japanese Deep South, and <strong>&#8216;The Last Picture Show&#8217;</strong> sees the start of his corruption as he helps a Yakuza neighbour with some rather dubious drug deals.  <strong>&#8216;The Wild Angels&#8217;</strong> then showcases his descent into debauchery, with scenes of orgies, drugs and randy GIs which could have come straight from <em>Almost Transparent Blue</em>.  In <strong>&#8216;La Dolce Vita&#8217;</strong>, we see our young friend come out of the other side of this storm as he returns to a more &#8216;normal life&#8217; at university (a story à la Haruki rather than <span class="st">Ryū</span>!).</p>
<p><em><strong>Swans</strong></em> takes us in a different direction again as, the title story aside (which features a random lesbian encounter at a Dutch-themed amusement park&#8230;), the common thread to the stories is a woman called Mieko.  The three remaining stories are all narrated by men who become involved with the rather unhappy woman.  The best of these is, perhaps, the first, <strong>&#8216;Historia de un Amor&#8217;</strong>, in which a young waiter connects with her over a shared passion for Cuban dance and music, and a rejection of conventional Japanese society:</p>
<p><em><strong>This society is steeped in the notion that we have everything we need.  I know, because I was steeped in it too until I met Jun&#8217;ichi.  The sad part is that people don&#8217;t like it when you begin to realize that something important is missing.  You can end up being ostracized, or even persecuted, like witches in the Middle Ages.</strong></em><br />
<strong>&#8216;Historia de un Amor&#8217;, p.192</strong></p>
<p>The meeting represents a chance of happiness for the unfortunate Mieko &#8211; the question is whether she&#8217;ll find the strength to take it.</p>
<p>The selection is rounded off by <strong>&#8216;At the Airport&#8217;</strong>, from the collection of the same name, where a woman (yet another prostitute) is waiting at the airport for her main client to take her on a trip to Hokkaido.  As people come and go, she stands reflecting on her life, lamenting the obstacles in the way of her dreams:</p>
<p><em><strong>I&#8217;m a high-school graduate, thirty-three years old, divorced, raising a four-year-old child, and I work in the sex trade.  These facts define who I am and limit my freedom and possibilities.</strong></em><br />
<strong>&#8216;At the Airport&#8217;, p.262</strong></p>
<p>The man she&#8217;s waiting for takes a different view, though, and if things go well, it might just be possible to follow her dreams anyway.  This is a bitter-sweet tale, and a nice way to end the book.</p>
<p>While publishing a best-of collection is an interesting idea, it would have been nice to see the rest of the stories, in particular, the thematically-linked pieces of <em>Run, Takahashi!</em> and <em>Swans</em>.  Perhaps, though, it&#8217;s better this way as some of the stories (particularly those from <em>Topaz</em>) did seem a little similar, which may have got a little old very quickly.  This approach also allows us to see Murakami at different stages of career, showing us his development as a writer and the change in focus over the years.</p>
<p>In short, <em>Tokyo Decadence</em> is an entertaining set of stories, well worth seeking out for all J-Lit lovers (and a must for any <span class="st">Ryū</span> fans out there), and if that sounds like your kind of thing, you should check out Kurodahan&#8217;s site for more.  They have a good range of books over a number of genres (e.g. Speculative Fiction and Thrillers), so there&#8217;s a good chance you&#8217;ll find something that appeals <span class='wp-smiley wp-emoji wp-emoji-smile' title=':)'>:)</span></p>
					</div><!-- .entry-content -->

	
	<footer class="entry-meta entry-footer">
		<span class="cat-links"><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/category/january-in-japan/" rel="category tag">January in Japan</a>, <a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/category/japan/" rel="category tag">Japan</a>, <a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/category/kurodahan-press/" rel="category tag">Kurodahan Press</a>, <a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/category/review/" rel="category tag">Review</a>, <a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/category/ryu-murakami/" rel="category tag">Ryu Murakami</a></span>	</footer><!-- .entry-footer -->
</article><!-- #post-5198 -->
			
				
<article id="post-5173" class="post-5173 post type-post status-publish format-standard hentry category-javier-marias category-review category-spain category-your-face-tomorrow">
	<header class="entry-header">

		<h2 class="entry-title"><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2016/02/01/your-face-tomorrow-3-poison-shadow-and-farewell-by-javier-marias-review/" rel="bookmark">&#8216;Your Face Tomorrow 3 &#8211; Poison, Shadow and Farewell&#8217; by Javier Marías&nbsp;(Review)</a></h2>
					<div class="entry-meta">
				<span class="posted-on"><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2016/02/01/your-face-tomorrow-3-poison-shadow-and-farewell-by-javier-marias-review/" rel="bookmark"><time class="entry-date published" datetime="2016-02-01T06:00:56+00:00">February 1, 2016</time><time class="updated" datetime="2016-01-31T17:30:32+00:00">January 31, 2016</time></a></span><span class="byline"><span class="author vcard"><a class="url fn n" href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/author/tonysreadinglist/">Tony</a></span></span><span class="comments-link"><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2016/02/01/your-face-tomorrow-3-poison-shadow-and-farewell-by-javier-marias-review/#comments">9 Comments</a></span>			</div><!-- .entry-meta -->
		
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	
		<div class="entry-content">
			<p><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.files.wordpress.com/2016/02/img_5385.jpg" rel="attachment wp-att-5193"><img class="size-medium wp-image-5193 alignleft" src="https://tonysreadinglist.files.wordpress.com/2016/02/img_5385.jpg?w=300&#038;h=225" alt="IMG_5385" width="300" height="225" /></a>As regular readers will know, a major part of my reading over the past couple of months has been devoted to <strong><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/category/javier-marias/">Javier Marías</a></strong> and his <em><strong><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/category/your-face-tomorrow/">Your Face Tomorrow</a></strong></em> trilogy.  The first part was a slow, measured build up, with the intriguing (and occasionally violent) middle section ramping up the tension.  So, with the third part bringing the 1400-page epic to a close, does Marías manage to finish off the story in style?  What do you think? <span class='wp-smiley wp-emoji wp-emoji-wink' title=';)'>;)</span></p>
<p><em><strong>Warning &#8211; if you don&#8217;t want to find out about what happened in the first two books, stop reading now <span class='wp-smiley wp-emoji wp-emoji-smile' title=':)'>:)</span></strong></em></p>
<p>*****<br />
<em><strong>Poison, Shadow and Farewell</strong></em> (translated by <strong>Margaret Jull Costa</strong>, published by <strong>Chatto &amp; Windus</strong>) begins with our Spanish friend Jacques Deza being driven to the house of his boss, the enigmatic Bertram Tupra.  The two arrive in darkness, proceeding to continue their conversation over drinks&#8230; and then, abruptly, we are taken back to Jacques&#8217; flat (to the point at which we left it at the end of Part 1!).  Taking up Deza&#8217;s interrupted conversation with Pérez Nuix, we learn of the story behind her request, and a whole lot more besides.</p>
<p>By the time we return to Tupra&#8217;s house, where the two men settle down to watch a rather disturbing DVD, the direction of the rest of the book is gradually becoming clearer, and Jacques&#8217; eventual return to Spain feels like a culmination of the events depicted so far.  After pages of musings on the theory of violence, and then a rather unexpected practical demonstration, the time has come for the apprentice to put what he has learned into practice &#8211; if he can bring himself to do it.  For the question that pervades the book comes into focus during this visit to Madrid, with Deza having to decide whether, if we are provoked enough, the end always justifies the means&#8230;</p>
<p><em>Poison, Shadow and Farewell</em> is an excellent way to end the series, a dismount with a double-tuck and triple-pike effortlessly nailed.  At around 550 pages, it&#8217;s far longer than the first two parts, but gripping from start to finish, providing ample pay-off for all the hard work the reader and writer have put in.  Marías (with the help of Tupra) slowly undermines our beliefs, making us question our certainty as to the way the world works, guiding us towards the eventual showdown.</p>
<p>The overriding theme of the book is violence, and after the scene in the nightclub toilets, Deza&#8217;s horror is scarcely contained.  He condemns Tupra&#8217;s actions, spluttering that you can&#8217;t just go around beating people up, but his boss has a surprise in store.  After showing Deza the DVD, one full of the horrendous depths of human nature, Tupra turns the Spaniard&#8217;s disgust back on to him:</p>
<p><em><strong>&#8220;You&#8217;ve had plenty of time to think about it, so answer the question I asked you in the car.  Now that you&#8217;ve seen things you&#8217;d never seen before and, I hope, never will again.  Tell me now, why, according to you, one can&#8217;t go around beating people up and killing them?  You&#8217;ve seen how much of it goes on, everywhere, and sometimes with an utter lack of concern.  So explain to me why one can&#8217;t.&#8221;</strong></em><br />
<strong>p.166 (Chatto &amp; Windus, 2009)</strong></p>
<p>As hard as Deza tries, a convincing answer remains elusive.  Perhaps the truth is that there is nothing to stop us maiming and killing at will after all&#8230;</p>
<p>This revelation marks a turning point in Deza&#8217;s life, and it&#8217;s time for him to decide whether this new life is, as he claims, just a &#8216;parenthesis&#8217; or his true calling.  This will be decided  on his return to Madrid when many of the earlier ideas are finally drawn together (his dreams, his memories of Luisa, even his neighbour) in the form of a potential danger to his estranged wife:</p>
<p><em><strong>That was something I had learned from Tupra, at least in theory: Luisa was clearly in danger, and now I understood that sometimes one has no option but to do what has to be done and at once, without waiting or hesitating or delaying&#8230; (</strong></em><strong>p.276)</strong></p>
<p>The scene is set, the players in place &#8211; now is the time to act, swiftly and mercilessly, no lingering, no delay.</p>
<p>There are few works I&#8217;ve read which are as skilfully constructed as <em>Your Face Tomorrow</em>, a book with scarcely a word in the wrong place.  Every passing mention, every little idea is woven into the fabric of the novel, all serving a greater purpose, with minor details recurring, gently jogging our memory.  As suspected, even the drop of blood mentioned in the first part has a much deeper significance than we could have imagined (on many levels), not least because, as you might have suspected by now, yes, there will be blood &#8211; and lots of it.</p>
<p>Blood is not something most readers will be comfortable with, and Marías discusses this in the comparisons of war and peace.  As Deza learns, it&#8217;s almost impossible to understand what is acceptable in one situation from the standpoint of the other.  The teachers here are the two old men of the novel, and while Jacques&#8217; father draws on the Spanish Civil War, retired spy Peter Wheeler finally lets Deza in on some of the secrets he&#8217;s been keeping regarding his actions during World War Two:</p>
<p><strong><em>&#8220;Don&#8217;t forget, Jacobo, the Second World War felt like a battle for survival.  And it was, it really was.  And in wars like that the limits on what one can acceptably do are constantly broadening out, almost without one realising it.  Times of peace judge times of war very harshly, and I&#8217;m not sure how far it&#8217;s possible to make such a judgement&#8230;&#8221;</em> (p.480)</strong></p>
<p>Again, we are confronted with the dilemma of the ends justifying the means, one we are only too familiar with in our modern era.  Not sure about that?  Ever heard of waterboarding, rendition?</p>
<p>As you will have gathered from my series of posts, I loved reading this book, a magnificent novel which builds on the writer&#8217;s previous work and extends it.  There are more Shakespearean echoes, naturally, with several themes repeated from <em><strong><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2015/09/24/tomorrow-in-the-battle-think-on-me-by-javier-marias-review/">Tomorrow in the Battle Think on Me</a></strong></em> and a plethora of  playful nods back to <em><strong><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2015/07/23/all-souls-by-javier-marias-review/">All Souls</a></strong></em>.  What&#8217;s even better, though, is what happens when Deza returns to Madrid, as we unexpectedly stumble across several links to another of his earlier novels, <em><strong><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2013/05/12/a-heart-so-white-by-javier-marias-review/">A Heart So White</a></strong></em> (be still my beating heart!).  I was tempted to race out to the library immediately and get a copy so that I could refresh my memory as to what Ranz and Custardoy got up to first time around.</p>
<p>This is a book, a whole trilogy in fact, that I feel sad to be returning to the library, and I definitely want my own set one day (in preparation for the inevitable reread).  The copy I borrowed has a sticker on the spine, a picture of a pistol, because it&#8217;s a &#8216;spy novel&#8217;, you see, but in truth it&#8217;s something much more than that, an examination of the nature of violence, the horrors of war and of knowing our limits and those of others.  I highly recommend you make time in your schedule to lose yourself in it &#8211; if not today, then perhaps tomorrow&#8230;</p>
					</div><!-- .entry-content -->

	
	<footer class="entry-meta entry-footer">
		<span class="cat-links"><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/category/javier-marias/" rel="category tag">Javier Marías</a>, <a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/category/review/" rel="category tag">Review</a>, <a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/category/spain/" rel="category tag">Spain</a>, <a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/category/your-face-tomorrow/" rel="category tag">Your Face Tomorrow</a></span>	</footer><!-- .entry-footer -->
</article><!-- #post-5173 -->
			
				
<article id="post-5162" class="post-5162 post type-post status-publish format-standard hentry category-monthly">
	<header class="entry-header">

		<h2 class="entry-title"><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2016/01/30/january-2016-wrap-up/" rel="bookmark">January 2016 Wrap-Up</a></h2>
					<div class="entry-meta">
				<span class="posted-on"><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2016/01/30/january-2016-wrap-up/" rel="bookmark"><time class="entry-date published" datetime="2016-01-30T12:00:54+00:00">January 30, 2016</time><time class="updated" datetime="2016-01-31T21:37:24+00:00">January 31, 2016</time></a></span><span class="byline"><span class="author vcard"><a class="url fn n" href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/author/tonysreadinglist/">Tony</a></span></span><span class="comments-link"><a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2016/01/30/january-2016-wrap-up/#comments">6 Comments</a></span>			</div><!-- .entry-meta -->
		
	</header><!-- .entry-header -->

	
		<div class="entry-content">
			<p>The start of a new year is always an exciting time, a blank slate and an opportunity to change <a href="https://tonysreadinglist.files.wordpress.com/2015/02/img_3100.jpg" rel="attachment wp-att-3188"><img class="size-medium wp-image-3188 alignright" src="https://tonysreadinglist.files.wordpress.com/2015/02/img_3100.jpg?w=184&#038;h=300" alt="January 2015 Wrap-Up" width="184" height="300" /></a>your approach.  One example of this in<strong> January</strong> was (sadly) my minimalist approach to <strong>January in Japan</strong>, eschewing a big blogging event in favour of a weekly post (which will probably continue into February).  Even with my recent decision to actively limit the number of posts I wrote, though, there were still a good number of books read and reviews posted this month.  Let&#8217;s check them out <span class='wp-smiley wp-emoji wp-emoji-smile' title=':)'>:)</span></p>
<p>*****<br />
Total Books Read: <strong>16</strong><br />
Year-to-Date: <strong>16</strong></p>
<p>New: <strong>12</strong><br />
Rereads: <strong>4</strong></p>
<p>From the Shelves: <strong>4</strong><br />
Review Copies: <strong>9</strong><br />
From the Library: <strong>2</strong><br />
On the Kindle: <strong>1</strong> (<strong>0 review copies</strong>)</p>
<p>Novels: <strong>6</strong><br />
Novellas: <strong>3</strong><br />
Short Stories: <strong>3</strong><br />
Non-Fiction: <strong>4</strong></p>
<p>Non-English Language: <strong>15</strong> (<strong>6 Japanese, 5 Spanish, French, Italian, Afrikaans, Korean</strong>)<br />
In Original Language: <strong>1</strong> (<strong>Spanish</strong>)</p>
<p>*****<br />
Books Reviewed in <strong>January</strong> were:<br />
<a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2016/01/04/the-old-garden-by-hwang-sok-yong-review/">1) <em>The Old Garden</em> by Hwang Sok-yong</a><br />
<a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2016/01/07/goodbye-tsugumi-by-banana-yoshimoto-review/">2) <em>Goodbye Tsugumi</em> by Banana Yoshimoto</a><br />
<a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2016/01/11/your-face-tomorrow-2-dance-and-dream-by-javier-marias-review/">3) <em>Your Face Tomorrow 2: Dance and Dream</em> by Javier Marías</a><br />
<a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2016/01/14/reading-the-tale-of-genji-sources-from-the-first-millennium-eds-thomas-harper-and-haruo-shirane-review/">4) <em>Reading the Tale of Genji</em> by Thomas Harper and Haruo Shirane (eds.)</a><br />
<a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2016/01/17/meet-at-the-ark-at-eight-by-ulrich-hub-review/">5) <em>Meet at the Ark at Eight!</em> by Ulrich Hub</a><br />
<a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2016/01/19/the-swan-whisperer-by-marlene-van-niekerk-translators-blues-by-franco-nasi-review/">6) <em>The Swan Whisperer</em> by Marlene Van Niekerk</a><br />
<a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2016/01/19/the-swan-whisperer-by-marlene-van-niekerk-translators-blues-by-franco-nasi-review/">7) <em>Translator&#8217;s Blues</em> by Franco Nasi</a><br />
<a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2016/01/21/ground-zero-nagasaki-by-yuichi-seirai-review/">8) <em>Ground Zero, Nagasaki</em> by Yūichi Seirai</a><br />
<a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2016/01/25/como-viajar-sin-ver-how-to-travel-without-seeing-by-andres-neuman-review/">9) <em>Cómo viajar sin ver (How to Travel Without Seeing)</em> by Andrés Neuman</a><br />
<a href="https://tonysreadinglist.wordpress.com/2016/01/28/death-by-water-by-kenzaburo-oe-review/">10) <em>Death by Water</em> by Kenzaburō Ōe</a></p>
<p>Tony&#8217;s Turkey for <strong>January</strong> is: <strong>Nothing</strong></p>
<p>An excellent start to the new year, with a collection of entertaining, high-standard books <span class='wp-smiley wp-emoji wp-emoji-smile' title=':)'>:)</span></p>
<p>Tony&#8217;s Recommendation for <strong>January</strong> is:<br />
<strong>Kenzaburō Ōe&#8217;s <em>Death by Water<br />
</em></strong></p>
<p>It is January in Japan, of course, so it shouldn&#8217;t come as a surprise to see a Japanese book chosen, and I enjoyed all four of my J-Lit selections this month.  Marías was desperately unlucky to be overlooked (again&#8230;); perhaps he&#8217;ll have better luck next month when the final part of the trilogy is to be reviewed on the blog.  However, Ōe&#8217;s novel about a man thwarted in his ambition to write a late-career classic is, ironically, a late-career classic and a worthy recipient of my monthly award <span class='wp-smiley wp-emoji wp-emoji-smile' title=':)'>:)</span></p>
<p>*****<br />
<strong>February</strong> looks like being more of the same.  I&#8217;ll be continuing to cover some Japanese books in my Thursday posts, with an assorted selection of other stuff in between.  Let&#8217;s see what catches my eye next month <span class='wp-smiley wp-emoji wp-emoji-smile' title=':)'>:)</span></p>
					</div><!-- .entry-content -->
